Another Chain for the Praise of Abu Musa
Ibn Asakir provides a second chain for the hadith praising Abu Musa's recitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of multiple chains of transmission in hadith and the specific chain for the hadith praising Abu Musa's recitation through 'A'ishah.

Core Idea
Multiple chains strengthen a hadith's authenticity.

This passage presents a second chain for a well-known hadith about Abu Musa al-Ash'ari's beautiful recitation. The Prophet compared his voice to the mazamir of David. Ibn Asakir documents this alternative route through 'A'ishah, demonstrating the meticulous care scholars took to preserve and authenticate prophetic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the chain and matn
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the long list of narrators before the matn of the hadith.
Notice the multiple shaykhs at the beginning, all reporting the same chain.
2
Identify the key narrators
Trace the chain from the shaykhs back to the Prophet. Identify al-Zuhri, 'Urwah, and 'A'ishah.
This chain is considered strong due to the reliable narrators.
3
Reflect on the matn
Read the Prophet's statement about Abu Musa's recitation. What does 'mizmar from the mazamir of David' mean?
It praises the beauty of Quranic recitation, not music.
4
Understand the significance
Consider why Ibn Asakir included a second chain. How does it strengthen the hadith?
Multiple chains increase confidence in the report's authenticity.

The shaykhs Abu Sa'd Isma'il ibn Ahmad ibn 'Abd al-Malik al-Faqih al-Kawmani in Baghdad, Abu al-Qasim Zahir ibn Tahir, Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-'Abbas ibn Ahmad al-Shaqani, Ahmad ibn Sahl ibn Ibrahim al-Masjidi, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Husayn ibn 'Ali al-Dar'aqili, Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Mansur ibn Abi Nasr al-Haradi, Abu Sa'd Mas'ud ibn Abi Sa'd ibn Abi 'Abd Allah al-Sha'ri, and others in Nishapur, and Abu 'Amr Isma'il ibn al-Husayn ibn Abi 'Amr (grandson of Ya'qub al-Adib al-Naysaburi) in Merv, all informed us, saying: Abu Bakr Ya'qub ibn Ahmad al-Sayrafi reported to us, Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Makhildi reported to us, Abu al-'Abbas Muhammad ibn Ishaq al-Sarraj reported to us, Ishaq ibn Ibrahim al-Hanzali reported to us, 'Abd al-Razzaq reported to us, Ma'mar reported to us, from al-Zuhri, from 'Urwah, from 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her), who said: The Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) heard the recitation of Abu al-Ash'ari Musa while he was reciting in the mosque, and he said: 'Indeed this one has been given a mizmar from the mazamir of David.' This is a hasan sahih hadith.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage provides another chain of transmission for the same hadith about Abu Musa's recitation, this time through 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her). The chain includes multiple shaykhs transmitting from Ibn al-Sarraj, who takes it from Ishaq ibn Ibrahim (Ibn Rahawayh), then 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ma'mar, al-Zuhri, 'Urwah, and 'A'ishah. The matn is similar: the Prophet heard Abu Musa reciting in the mosque and said, 'Indeed this one has been given a mizmar from the mazamir of David.' The narrator notes that this hadith is hasan sahih (good and authentic). This multiplicity of chains strengthens the reliability of the report.

Key Terms
أبو موسى
مزامير داود
عائشة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith is about singing or musical instruments.
Correction: The Prophet's statement refers to the beauty of Quranic recitation, not music. 'Mizmar' metaphorically means a beautiful voice, and David was known for his melodious recitation of the Psalms.

Checkpoint

A) Abu Hurairah
B) 'A'ishah
C) Ibn 'Umar
D) Anas

Key Takeaway
Multiple chains of transmission increase the authenticity of a hadith, as demonstrated by Ibn Asakir's recording of a second chain for the praise of Abu Musa's recitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This hadith was transmitted through multiple chains to ensure its authenticity. In your own religious learning, do you seek multiple sources and verify information, or do you accept things without checking? How can you apply the principle of multiple chains to your personal study?
?
The Prophet noticed Abu Musa's recitation while he was praying in the mosque. Are you attentive when the Qur'an is recited, or do you become distracted? How can you train your heart to listen with the same awareness as the Prophet?

The Prophet's Instructions to Abu Musa and Mu'adh: Gentle Da'wah
Ibn Asakir narrates the Prophet's advice to Abu Musa and Mu'adh when sending them to Yemen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prophetic methodology of da'wah based on this hadith: giving glad tidings, making things easy, teaching, and avoiding harshness.

Core Idea
The Prophet's da'wah method: glad tidings, not repulsion.

This lesson examines a key prophetic instruction given to two companions sent to Yemen. In just a few words, the Prophet outlined the core principles of Islamic propagation — gentleness, facilitation, and harmony. This hadith is a foundational text for understanding the proper approach in inviting others to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic hadith with its chain. Notice the repetition of the chain indicators (ḥ). Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
The chain shows the reliability of the transmission.
2
Identify the four commands
Find the four instructions given by the Prophet: bishshira (give glad tidings), yassira (make easy), 'allima (teach), la tunaffira (do not make averse). Reflect on why each is important.
The order is significant: glad tidings first.
3
Consider the additional phrase about harmony
The narrator mentions 'wa tatawa'a' (and be in harmony). Think about why mutual harmony matters among missionaries.
Some narrations include this, others do not.
4
Connect to the broader prophetic methodology
Relate these instructions to other hadiths and Quranic verses about gentle da'wah. Understand that this is not about compromising the message, but about the manner of delivery.
Common misunderstanding avoided.
5
Apply the lesson to your own life
Think of a situation where you can implement these principles: being positive, making learning easy, teaching patiently, and avoiding harshness.
This is a practical lesson for all Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم على بن إبراهيم الحسيني أنا أبو الحسين محمد بن عبدالرحمن بن عثمان التميمي، أنا أبوبكر يوسف بن القسم الميانجي ح وأخبرنا أبو عبد الله محمد بن الفضل وأبو المظفر عبد المنعم بن عبد الكريم النيسابوريان قالا أنا أبو سعد محمد بن عبدالرحمن بن محمد الجنزوري، أنا أبو عمرو محمد بن أحمد بن حمدان الحيرى ح وأخبرنا أبو عبد الله الحسين بن عبد الملك أنا إبراهيم بن منصور السلمي أخبرنا أبوبكر ابن المقرى قالوا نا أبو يعلى الموصلى نا محمد بن عباد المکی نا سفيان عن عمرو سمعه عن سعيد بن أبي بردة عن أبيه عن جده أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بعثه ومعاذا إلى اليمن فقال لهما بشرا ويسرا وعلما ولا تنفرا) وأراه قال ( وتطاوعا) الحديث
The noble sharif Abu al-Qasim 'Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Husayni informed us, Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Uthman al-Tamimi reported to us, Abu Bakr Yusuf ibn al-Qasim al-Miyanaji [ḥ] (change) and Abu 'Abd Allah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl and Abu al-Muzaffar 'Abd al-Mun'im ibn 'Abd al-Karim al-Naysaburiyan both said: Abu Sa'd Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad al-Janzi reported to us, Abu 'Amr Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Hamdan al-Hayri [ḥ] (change) and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Husayn ibn 'Abd al-Malik informed us, Ibrahim ibn Mansur al-Sulami reported to us, Abu Bakr ibn al-Muqri' reported to us, they all said: Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili reported to us, Muhammad ibn 'Abbad al-Makki reported to us, Sufyan reported to us, from 'Amr who heard from Sa'id ibn Abi Burdah, from his father, from his grandfather, that the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) sent him and Mu'adh to Yemen and said to them: 'Give glad tidings, make things easy, teach, and do not make things repulsive.' And I think he said: 'And be in harmony.' The hadith.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith with multiple chains (indicated by 'ḥ' for tahwil). The matn reports that when the Prophet sent Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Mu'adh ibn Jabal to Yemen, he gave them key instructions: 'Give glad tidings (bishshira), make things easy (yassira), teach ('allima), and do not make people averse (la tunaffira).' The narrator adds that he thinks the Prophet also said 'and be in harmony (wa tatawa'a).' This hadith emphasizes the importance of gentleness, facilitation, and avoiding harshness in da'wah and teaching, which is a fundamental principle in Islamic propagation.

Key Terms
بشرا ويسرا
علما ولا تنفرا
تطاوعا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this hadith teaches to compromise Islamic teachings to make people happy.
Correction: The hadith is about the manner of delivery, not the content. The message remains firm, but the approach is gentle.

Checkpoint
What did the Prophet instruct Abu Musa and Mu'adh to do?
A) Give glad tidings, make things easy, teach, and do not make people averse
B) Give warnings, make things difficult, and enforce strict rules
C) Teach only the Quran and ignore other matters
D) Spread Islam by force

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's concise instructions provide a timeless framework for da'wah: be positive, facilitate, educate, avoid harshness, and maintain harmony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you invite others to Islam, do you start with glad tidings (bushra) or with warnings? Reflect on one recent conversation and what you will do differently next time to prioritize encouragement.
?
Think of a person who finds Islam difficult. What is one specific thing you can say or do today to 'make easy' (yusr) for them, without compromising the truth? Write down your action.
?
The Prophet commanded harmony (tatawu'a) between da'is. Is there a fellow Muslim worker you are not in harmony with? What concrete step will you take today to reconcile or cooperate better?

Command to Teach the Quran to Yemen
Ibn Asakir narrates that the Prophet sent Abu Musa and Mu'adh to teach the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the priority of Quranic education as demonstrated by the Prophet's direct command to his companions.

Core Idea
The Prophet's first instruction to Yemen was Quranic education.

This lesson presents a Prophetic tradition that highlights the priority of Quranic teaching. In this hadith, the Prophet sends two companions to Yemen with a clear command: teach the Quran. This establishes the foundational role of scholars in spreading divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the chain and text. Note the names and the command.
2
Examine the chain of narration
Identify the narrators: Abu Musa, Abu Burdah, Talhah, etc. Understand that this is a reliable chain through Imam Ahmad.
The chain includes multiple trustworthy narrators.
3
Focus on the command
The Prophet commanded them to teach the people the Quran. Reflect on why this was the first instruction.
Teaching the Quran includes its meanings and rulings.
4
Compare the two versions
Note the alternative wording: 'teach the people the matters of their religion.' This expands the scope to include all religious knowledge.
Both versions emphasize education.
5
Apply the lesson
Consider how this hadith guides us today in prioritizing Quranic learning and teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم هبة الله بن محمد بن الحصين أنا أبو على الحسن بن على بن المذهب أنا أبوبكر أحمد بن جعفر بن حمدان نا عبدالله ابن أحمد بن حنبل، حدثني أبي نا عبد الله بن نمير عن طلحة أخبرني أبو بردة عن أبي موسى رضى الله عنه أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعث معاذا وأبا موسى إلى اليمن وأمرهما أن يعلما الناس القرآن رواه غيره عن طلحة بن يحيى فقال عن أبي بردة عن أبي موسى ومعاذ حين بعثهما إلى اليمن يعلمان الناس أمر دينهم
The shaykh Abu al-Qasim Hibat Allah ibn Muhammad ibn al-Husayn informed us, Abu 'Ali al-Hasan ibn 'Ali ibn al-Madh-hab reported to us, Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ja'far ibn Hamdan reported to us, 'Abd Allah ibn Ahmad ibn Hanbal reported to us, my father narrated to me, 'Abd Allah ibn Numayr reported to us, from Talhah, Abu Burdah informed me, from Abu Musa (may Allah be pleased with him), that the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) sent Mu'adh and Abu Musa to Yemen and commanded them to teach the people the Quran. Others narrated it from Talhah ibn Yahya who said: from Abu Burdah, from Abu Musa and Mu'adh when he sent them to Yemen to teach the people the matters of their religion.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides another narration regarding the Prophet's sending of Abu Musa and Mu'adh to Yemen. The chain goes through 'Abd Allah ibn Ahmad ibn Hanbal from his father (Imam Ahmad), from 'Abd Allah ibn Numayr, from Talhah, from Abu Burdah, from Abu Musa. The matn states that the Prophet commanded them to teach the people the Quran. The narrator adds an alternative version from Talhah ibn Yahya, which mentions that they were to teach the people the matters of their religion (din). This hadith underscores the importance of Quranic education and the role of scholars in spreading religious knowledge.

Key Terms
بعث معاذا وأبا موسى
تعليم القرآن
أمر الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Teaching the Quran only means teaching its recitation without meaning.
Correction: The hadith emphasizes teaching the Quran, which includes its meanings and rulings, as reinforced by the alternative wording 'matters of their religion.'

Checkpoint

A) To collect taxes
B) To teach the people the Quran
C) To build mosques
D) To fight the disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's sending of companions to teach the Quran establishes the priority of sacred knowledge and the duty of scholars to educate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet personally sent you to teach the Quran today, would you feel prepared? What is one specific gap in your own Quranic learning that you must fill to be worthy of such a task?
?
The Prophet's first command to Yemen was education, not politics or warfare. In your daily priorities, does learning and teaching the Quran take precedence over worldly ambitions? If not, what must change starting this week?
?
The two versions of the hadith show that teaching the Quran includes teaching 'the matters of religion.' Are you content with only reciting the Quran without seeking its meanings and applying them? Confess one area where you have neglected to integrate Quranic guidance into your life.

Prophetic Da'wa Methodology: Gentleness and Facilitation
Hadith report with instructions for da'wa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Prophet's guidance for spreading Islam through gentleness, encouragement, and facilitation rather than harshness.

Core Idea
Gentle guidance wins hearts; harshness repels.

This hadith records the Prophet's instructions to two companions sent to Yemen as teachers and guides. It provides a timeless methodology for calling others to Islam. As you read the narration, notice the key phrases that summarize the prophetic approach.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The Prophet sent Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Mu'adh ibn Jabal to Yemen as teachers and guides. Recognize the importance of sending knowledgeable companions to spread Islam.
2
Identify the key instructions
The Prophet said: 'Give good news, make things easy, teach, and do not cause aversion.' These four commands form the core of da'wa methodology.
3
Reflect on cooperation
The narrator adds 'and cooperate' (tatawa'u). This highlights the importance of teamwork and mutual support in da'wa.
4
Connect to modern da'wa
Consider how these principles apply today: positive messaging, simplifying teachings, avoiding unnecessary restrictions, and working together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم علي بن إبراهيم الحسيني أنا أبو الحسين محمد بن عبد الرحمن بن عثمان التميمي، أنا أبوبكر يوسف بن القسم الميانجي ح وأخبرنا أبو عبد الله محمد بن الفضل وأبو المظفر عبد المنعم بن عبد الكريم النيسابوريان قالا أنا أبو سعد محمد بن عبد الرحمن بن محمد الجنزورذي، أنا أبو عمرو محمد بن أحمد بن حمدان الحيرى ح وأخبرنا أبو عبد الله الحسين بن عبد الملك أنا إبراهيم بن منصور السلمي أخبرنا أبوبكر ابن المقرئ قالوا نا أبو يعلى الموصلي نا محمد بن عباد المكي نا سفيان عن عمرو سمعه عن سعيد بن أبي بردة عن أبيه عن جده أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بعثه ومعاذا إلى اليمن فقال لهما بشرا ويسرا وعلما ولا تنفرا) وأراه قال ( وتطاوعا) الحديث،
The noble Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Husayni informed us, he said: Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Uthman al-Tamimi narrated to us, he said: Abu Bakr Yusuf ibn al-Qasim al-Miyanaji [ح] and Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl and Abu al-Muzaffar Abd al-Munim ibn Abd al-Karim al-Naysaburiyan both said: Abu Sa'd Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad al-Junzurudhi narrated to us, he said: Abu Amr Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Hamdan al-Hayri narrated to us [ح] and Abu Abdullah al-Husayn ibn Abd al-Malik informed us, he said: Ibrahim ibn Mansur al-Sulami informed us, he said: Abu Bakr ibn al-Muqri' informed us, they all said: Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili narrated to us, he said: Muhammad ibn Abbad al-Makki narrated to us, he said: Sufyan narrated to us from Amr who heard it from Sa'id ibn Abi Burda from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet (peace be upon him) sent him and Mu'adh to Yemen and said to them: 'Give good news, make things easy, teach, and do not cause aversion,' and I think he said: 'and cooperate.' The hadith.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a hadith report transmitted through multiple chains. It narrates that the Prophet (peace be upon him) sent both Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Mu'adh ibn Jabal to Yemen as teachers and guides. The key instruction is summarized in the phrases: 'Give good news (bishra), make things easy (yusr), teach (ta'lim), and do not cause aversion (la tunaffiru).' The narrator adds that he thinks the Prophet also said 'and cooperate (tatawa'u).' This hadith is foundational for da'wa methodology, emphasizing gentleness, positive encouragement, and facilitation rather than harshness. It also highlights the companions' role in spreading Islamic knowledge.

Key Terms
Bishra
Yusr
Ta'lim
Tatawu

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people believe that Islamic teaching must be harsh, stern, or confrontational to be effective.
Correction: This hadith explicitly commands gentleness and ease: 'Give good news, make things easy, teach, and do not cause aversion.' Harshness is counterproductive and against the prophetic method.

Checkpoint

A) Fight the disbelievers
B) Give good news, teach, and not cause aversion
C) Only teach Quran
D) Collect zakat

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's method for da'wa is based on encouragement, facilitation, and avoidance of aversion, not harshness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent conversation about Islam where you were more concerned with 'being right' than with 'being gentle'. How did that affect the other person? Will you ask their forgiveness today and change your approach?
?
The Prophet commanded 'do not cause aversion.' Is there anyone in your life—family, friend, or stranger—who feels distanced from Islam because of your words or actions? What specific step will you take this week to repair that, even if it means apologizing?
?
You have been taught to 'teach' and 'make things easy.' But when was the last time you truly celebrated someone's small step toward good? Are you more focused on correcting mistakes or encouraging progress? Challenge yourself to give one genuine compliment about someone's faith effort today.

The Prophetic Mission to Teach the Quran
Report on sending Mu'adh and Abu Musa to teach Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of teaching the Quran as a primary mission of the Prophet's companions, and how hadith variants preserve details.

Core Idea
Teaching the Quran was the first priority for new Muslim communities.

This brief yet significant report captures a moment where the Prophet specifically tasked two of his companions with educating the people of Yemen in the Quran. The report also shows how careful transmission preserves even slight variations in wording, reminding us that the Quran and the teachings of Islam go hand in hand.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the chain of narrators and the two versions.
The second version adds 'matters of their religion'.
2
Focus on the command
Identify the Prophet's command: to teach the people the Quran. Reflect on why this was a priority for new Muslims.
3
Compare the variants
Notice the difference between the two versions. What does the second version add? How does this enrich our understanding?
Both versions are preserved, showing the care of hadith scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Talha ibn Yahya said: The shaykh Abu al-Qasim Hibatullah ibn Muhammad ibn al-Husayn informed us, he said: Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn al-Madhhab narrated to us, he said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ja'far ibn Hamdan narrated to us, he said: Abdullah ibn Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated to us: my father narrated to me, he said: Abdullah ibn Numayr narrated to us from Talha, he said: Abu Burda narrated to me from Abu Musa (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sent Mu'adh and Abu Musa to Yemen and commanded them to teach the people the Quran. Commentary

The narrator notes another version that adds 'and to teach the matters of their religion.' This reinforces the educational mission of the companions. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Key Takeaway
The Prophet commanded teaching the Quran as the foundation of religious knowledge, and hadith variants enrich our understanding of his mission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The hadith shows the Prophet's meticulous care in spreading knowledge. When you learn Islam, are you seeking the kind of deep understanding that you could then teach others? Or are you content with surface-level knowledge? Commit to one action that moves you from passive learning to active comprehension.

The Prophet's Gentle Advice to Teach Sunnah
Narration of the Prophet's advice to Abu Musa and Mu'adh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Prophet's emphasis on gentleness and facilitation in spreading Islam and teaching the Sunnah.

Core Idea
Spread Islam with gentleness, not harshness.

This narration captures the Prophet's practical guidance for those entrusted with teaching and calling others to Islam. Before sending Abu Musa and Mu'adh to Yemen, he gave them a concise instruction that balances duty with compassion. The lesson highlights the Prophetic method of da'wah.

Guided Path
1
Read the narration and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the chain of narrators and the context of the Prophet's advice.
Pay attention to the phrase 'to teach them the Sunnah' – this is the primary mission.
2
Identify the key phrases of advice
Find the two commands: 'Give good news and do not cause aversion' and 'make things easy and do not make things difficult.' Reflect on their meaning.
These phrases are the core of the lesson.
3
Reflect on the importance of teaching Sunnah
Consider why the Prophet specifically mentioned teaching the Sunnah, not just the Quran, and how this instruction shapes Islamic education.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو سهل محمد بن إبراهيم المزكي أنا أبو الفضل عبد الرحمن بن أحمد بن الحسن الرازي المقرى نا أبو القسم جعفر بن بن فناكي الرازی نا أبوبكر محمد بن هرون الرویانی نا محمد بن معمرنا محمد بن بكر البرسانى نا إياس بن ذغفل نا سيار أبو الحكم سعيد بن أبي بردة عن أبي موسى أو عن أبي بردة عن أبي موسى رضى الله عنه قال أوصانا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حين بعثنا إلى اليمن أنا وبعاداً نعلمهم السنة قال فأوصانا حين أردنا نتوجه قال ( بشروا ولا تنفروا ويسروا ولا تعسروا) في حديث ذكره .
The shaykh Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Muzakki informed us, he said: Abu al-Fadl Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan al-Razi al-Muqri' narrated to us, he said: Abu al-Qasim Ja'far ibn bin Fanki al-Razi narrated to us, he said: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Harun al-Rawiyani narrated to us, he said: Muhammad ibn Mu'ammar narrated to us, he said: Muhammad ibn Bakr al-Barsani narrated to us, he said: Iyas ibn Dhaghfal narrated to us, he said: Sayyar Abu al-Hakam Sa'id ibn Abi Burda narrated to us, from Abu Musa or from Abu Burda from Abu Musa (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) advised us when he sent us to Yemen—myself and Mu'adh—to teach them the Sunnah. He said: He advised us when we intended to set out: 'Give good news and do not cause aversion, make things easy and do not make things difficult.' In a hadith he mentioned.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides another version of the Prophet's advice. It specifies that the instruction was to teach the Sunnah, not just the Quran. The key phrases are: 'Give good news (bashshiru) and do not cause aversion (la tunaffiru), make things easy (yassiru) and do not make things difficult (la tu'assiru).' This emphasizes a positive, gentle approach in teaching and spreading Islam. The report also shows the care in transmission with the phrase 'from Abu Musa or from Abu Burda from Abu Musa,' indicating doubt about the exact chain.

?

?
This hadith is directed to teachers, but you teach Islam by your actions every day. In your interactions, are you more likely to cause aversion or attraction to the deen? What one habit can you change to embody 'yusr' (ease) starting today?

Six Companions of Knowledge
Ibn Asakir reports al-Sha'bi's list of six early scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early Islamic knowledge was transmitted through a collective network of six key Companions, organized into two interacting groups.

Core Idea
Early Islamic scholarship was a shared enterprise among six Companions.

This lesson presents a report from the early scholar al-Sha'bi about the primary sources of knowledge from the Prophet's Companions. Ibn Asakir preserves this narration to highlight the interconnected nature of early Islamic learning. You will read the names, see how they were grouped, and grasp the collective character of knowledge transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the names and the grouping.
The Arabic contains a chain of narrators; focus on al-Sha'bi's statement.
2
Identify the six companions
List the six names mentioned: Umar, Abdullah, Zaid, Ali, Ubayy, and al-Ash'ari.
Abdullah likely refers to Ibn Mas'ud, Zaid to Zaid bin Thabit.
3
Notice the two groups
Al-Sha'bi divides them into two groups of three. Observe the description: each group's knowledge resembled one another and they borrowed from each other.
4
Understand the clarification about al-Ash'ari
When asked if al-Ash'ari was considered a jurist, al-Sha'bi confirms he was one of the jurists.
This emphasizes his scholarly rank.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters: it shows that early knowledge was not isolated but collaborative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخان ابو عبد الله يحيى بن الحسن بن أحمد بن البناء وابو القسم بن السمرقندى قالا انا ابو محمد عبد الله بن محمد الخطيب انا ابو حفص عمر بن ابراهيم بن أحمد بن كثير الکنانی نا عبدالله بن عبد العزيز نا ابو خيثمة نا عباد بن العوام عن الشيبانی یعنی ابا اسحق سليمان بن فيروز عن الشعبي قال : كان يؤخذ العلم عن ستة من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فكان عمر وعبد الله وزيد يشبه علمهم بعضهم بعضا وكان يقتبس بعضهم من بعض وكان على وأبى والأشعري يشبه علمهم بعضهم بعضا ويقتبس بعضهم من بعض قال فقلت له : وكان الأشعرى إلى هؤلاء قال كان أحد الفقهاء .
The two shaykhs informed us... from al-Sha'bi who said: Knowledge was taken from six (companions) of the Messenger of Allah. Umar, Abdullah, and Zaid – their knowledge resembled one another and they used to borrow from one another. Ali, Ubayy, and al-Ash'ari – their knowledge resembled one another and they used to borrow from one another. He said: I said to him: Was al-Ash'ari among these? He said: He was one of the jurists.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a biographical report about the transmission of knowledge from the Prophet's companions. Ibn Asakir narrates through a chain to al-Sha'bi, who lists six companions from whom knowledge was taken: Umar, Abdullah (likely Ibn Mas'ud), Zaid (bin Thabit), Ali, Ubayy (bin Ka'b), and al-Ash'ari (Abu Musa al-Ash'ari). He then divides them into two groups: the first three (Umar, Abdullah, Zaid) had similar knowledge and borrowed from each other; the second three (Ali, Ubayy, al-Ash'ari) also had similar knowledge and borrowed from each other. When asked whether al-Ash'ari was among these jurists, al-Sha'bi confirms he was one of the jurists. The passage emphasizes the interconnectedness and mutual learning among these early authorities, highlighting the collective nature of early Islamic scholarship.

Key Terms
ستة
علم
أشعري
فقهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari refers to the theologian Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.
Correction: It refers to the companion Abu Musa al-Ash'ari.

Checkpoint
How many companions are listed by al-Sha'bi as sources of knowledge?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
Early Islamic knowledge was transmitted through a collaborative network of six key Companions, grouped into two sets of mutual learners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even the greatest Companions learned from each other. Where in your life are you resisting collaboration, thinking you can master knowledge alone? What step will you take this week to 'borrow' knowledge from a fellow learner?
?
Al-Sha'bi lists six names—ordinary men turned into authorities by their humility to learn. Does your pride prevent you from acknowledging that others may have insights you lack? Write down one person you can ask for guidance today.
?
The report emphasizes mutual borrowing (يَقْتَبِسُ). Are you actively sharing what you know, or are you hoarding knowledge out of insecurity? Commit to teaching one thing you learned recently to someone else before the week ends.

Only Four Companions Gave Fatwa in the Prophet’s Mosque
Ibn Asakir narrates that only four companions gave fatwa in the Prophet's mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that only four Companions were authorized to issue fatwas in the Prophet’s mosque during his lifetime, highlighting the careful control of religious authority.

Core Idea
Only four Companions were authorized to give fatwa in the Prophet's mosque.

This lesson presents a short narration from Safwan ibn Sulaym that narrows down the circle of those who issued legal opinions in the Prophet’s mosque. It shows that religious authority was tightly controlled and that only Umar, Ali, Muadh, and Abu Musa held this privilege. This report sheds light on the high scholarly standing of these four Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the narration carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the names mentioned and the context of the Prophet's mosque during his time.
The chain of narration is long but authentic.
2
Identify the four names
List the four Companions: Umar, Ali, Muadh, and Abu Musa. Reflect on what you know about each of them.
3
Understand the significance
Ask yourself why only these four were allowed to give fatwa. This shows the early community's emphasis on qualified scholarship.
This connects to the lesson's learning objective.
4
Consider the broader implication
Think about how this narration supports the high status of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, who is often known as a companion from Yemen.
This ties to the previous lesson's mention of six main sources of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ ابو على الحسن بن أحمد بن الحسن المقرى فى كتابه إلى من اصبهان وحدثني الشيخ ابو مسعود عبد الرحيم بن على بن حمد المعدل باصبهان عنه انا ابو نعيم أحمد بن عبدالله بن أحمد الحافظ نا محمد بن أحمد بن الحسن، نا محمد ابن عثمان بن أبي شيبة نا سعيد بن عمرو وهو الأشعثى انا حاتم بن اسماعيل عن اسامة بن زيد عن صفوان بن سليم قال : لم يكن يفتي في مسجد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم زمن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم غير هؤلاء القوم عمر وعلى ومعاذ وأبو موسى رضى الله عنهم،
The shaykh Abu Ali informed us... from Safwan ibn Sulaym who said: None gave fatwa in the mosque of the Messenger of Allah during the time of the Messenger of Allah except these people: Umar, Ali, Mu'adh, and Abu Musa – may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a historical report about the restricted circle of those who issued fatwas (legal opinions) in the Prophet's mosque during his lifetime. The chain goes back to Safwan ibn Sulaym, who states that only four companions performed this role: Umar ibn al-Khattab, Ali ibn Abi Talib, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. This highlights the high scholarly standing of these companions and the careful control over religious authority in the early Muslim community. It also underscores the prominent position of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari as a jurist, which is consistent with the previous lesson's mention of him as one of the six main sources of knowledge.

Key Terms

زمن
فتوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many Companions gave fatwa in the Prophet's mosque.
Correction: Only four specifically named Companions were authorized to give fatwa during the Prophet's lifetime.

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Only four Companions—Umar, Ali, Muadh, and Abu Musa—issued fatwas in the Prophet's mosque, emphasizing the early community's rigorous control over religious authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek religious guidance, do you prioritize the authority of the person giving it? This lesson shows that even in the Prophet's mosque, fatwa was limited to four trusted companions. How do you verify the credibility of those you learn from? Will you commit today to checking the sources of your Islamic knowledge?
?
The four companions were chosen for their deep understanding. Do you feel your own knowledge of Islam is reliable enough to make important decisions? What specific step will you take this week to strengthen your understanding of the Quran and Sunnah?
?
This passage reveals the early community's careful control over religious authority. Are you ever too quick to accept a teaching that is popular but unverified? How can you ensure that the fatwas you follow come from a solid foundation? Will you ask for evidence before accepting a ruling?

Abu Musa's Mission: Teaching and Civic Duty
Ibn Asakir reports Abu Musa's statement upon arriving in Basra sent by Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Abu Musa's mission to Basra combined religious education (Quran and Sunnah) with practical civic responsibilities, reflecting the holistic nature of early Islamic governance.

Core Idea
A governor's duty includes both teaching the Quran and cleaning the streets.

This brief report from Imam Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kidhb al-Muftari narrates the mission of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari to Basra as sent by Umar ibn al-Khattab. The passage highlights three distinct tasks assigned to Abu Musa, showing that his role was not limited to religious instruction but also encompassed public works. This reflects the comprehensive approach of early Muslim leaders, who combined spiritual guidance with worldly administration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of narration and then the direct quote from Abu Musa.
The chain includes many narrators, but the key statement is from Abu Musa himself.
2
Identify the three tasks
Find the three things Abu Musa says Umar sent him to do. List them in order.
The tasks are: teaching the Book of your Lord, teaching the Sunnah of your Prophet, and cleaning your streets.
3
Reflect on the third task
Consider why cleaning the streets is included alongside teaching. What does this imply about the role of an early Muslim governor?
This shows that civic administration and public welfare were part of the religious mission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيوخ أبو الفضل محمد بن اسماعيل بن الفضيل الفضيلي وأبو المحاسن أسعد بن على بن الموفق بن زياد الحنفى وأبو الوقت عبد الأول بن عيسى بن شعيب السجزى وأبو بكر احمد بن يحيى ابن الحسن الاذربيجاني بهراة قالوا انا أبو الحسن عبدالرحمن بن محمد بن المظفر الداودي ببوشنج، أنا أبو محمد عبدالله بن احمد بن حمويه السرخسى انا أبو عمران عيسى بن عمر بن العباس السمرقندى انا أبو محمد عبد الله بن عبدالرحمن الدارمی نا عبيد بن يعيش نا يونس عن صالح بن رستم المزنى عن الحسن عن أبي موسى انه قال حين قدم البصرة : بعثني اليكم عمر بن الله الخطاب رضى عنه اعلمكم كتاب ربكم وسنة نبيكم صلى الله عليه وسلم وأنظف طزقكم،
The shaykhs informed us... from al-Hasan from Abu Musa that he said when he arrived in Basra: Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, sent me to you to teach you the Book of your Lord, the Sunnah of your Prophet, and to clean your streets.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a report about the mission of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari to Basra. Through a chain to al-Hasan al-Basri, Abu Musa himself states the three tasks Umar assigned him: teaching the Quran (the Book of your Lord), teaching the Sunnah (the practice of the Prophet), and cleaning the streets. The third task, 'أنظف طرقكم' (clean your streets), indicates that his responsibilities included not only religious education but also civic administration and public works. This highlights the comprehensive role of early Muslim governors and scholars in both spiritual and worldly affairs.

Misconception: One might think that Umar's mission for Abu Musa was purely religious, focused only on teaching Quran and Sunnah.
Correction: The passage explicitly includes cleaning streets as a third task, demonstrating that early Islamic governance combined spiritual and worldly duties.

Checkpoint

B) Teach Quran, teach Sunnah, and clean streets

D) Teach Quran, teach Sunnah, and fight enemies

Key Takeaway
The mission of Abu Musa illustrates that Islamic scholarship and governance are intertwined, encompassing both spiritual and worldly duties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Musa was sent to teach and clean streets. In your life, do you treat 'spiritual' tasks and 'mundane' tasks as equally sacred, or do you neglect practical duties for the sake of 'religious' activities? What change will you make today?
?
When you think of leadership, do you imagine grand speeches or sweeping streets? Which of these do you shy away from, and why?
?
Umar considered street cleaning a governor's duty. What lowly task in your community are you ignoring because you think it's beneath you? Will you commit to doing it this week?

Umar's detailed instructions to Abu Musa for Basra
Ibn Asakir reports Umar's instructions to Abu Musa to teach, fight, and distribute spoils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive mission Umar assigned to Abu Musa in Basra, covering education, military leadership, and governance.

Core Idea
Umar appointed Abu Musa to be a teacher, commander, and governor all in one.

This passage from Ibn Asakir provides a vivid example of how the early Muslim leaders integrated education, military, and governance into a single mission. Umar's instructions to Abu Musa reveal the holistic approach to leadership in early Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the full narrative of Umar's commissioning and al-Hasan's testimony.
2
Identify Umar's two 'needs'
Notice the distinction Umar makes between Abu Musa's personal desire (jihad) and his own need for him in Basra.
Umar says 'so that you prefer my need over your need'.
3
List the three duties
Find the three tasks Umar enumerates: teaching the Quran and Sunnah, fighting the enemy, and distributing spoils.
4
Appreciate the outcome
Read al-Hasan's testimony that Abu Musa fulfilled all three duties, and the description of his daily practice after Fajr.
Ibn Shudhab describes how he would go row by row teaching the Quran.
5
Reflect on the camel detail
Consider the significance of Abu Musa entering and leaving on a grey camel as a sign of simplicity and lack of material accumulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبد الله محمد بن الفضل الفقيه انا أبو بكر احمد ابن الحسين البيهقى وأخبرنا أبو القسم ابن السمرقندي أخبرنا أبو بكر محمد بن هبة الله الطبرى قالا انا محمد بن الحسين بن الفضل ببغداد انا عبد الله بن جعفر نا يعقوب بن سفيان حدثنا سعيد بن اسد نا ضمرة عن ابن شوذب عن الحسن قال بعث عمر بن الخطاب رضى الله عنه إلي أبى موسى الأشعرى رضى الله عنه وهو بالشام فقدم عليه فلما قدم عليه قال له : انى انما بعثت إليك لخير لتؤثر حاجتى على حاجتك اما حاجتك فالجهاد في سبيل الله واما حاجتي فأبعثك إلى البصرة فتعلمهم كتاب ربهم وسنة نبيهم وتجاهد بهم عدوهم وتقسم بينهم فيئهم، قال الحسن رحمه الله فاعل والله لقد علمهم كتاب ربهم وسنة نبيهم وجاهد بهم عدوهم، وقسم بينهم فيئهم فوالله ماقدم عليهم راكب كان خيرا لهم من أبي موسى الأشعرى، قال ابن شوذب كان إذا صلى الصبح أمر الناس فثبتوا في مجالسهم ثم استقبل الصفوف رجلا رجلا يقرئه القرآن حتى يأتي على الصفوف قال ابن شوذب ودخل على جمل اورق وخرج عليه حين عزل،
The shaykh informed us... from al-Hasan who said: Umar ibn al-Khattab sent for Abu Musa al-Ash'ari while he was in Syria. He came to him, and when he arrived, he said to him: I only sent for you for good – that you prefer my need over your need. As for your need, it is jihad in the way of Allah. As for my need, I am sending you to Basra to teach them the Book of their Lord, the Sunnah of their Prophet, to fight alongside them against their enemy, and to distribute the spoils among them. Al-Hasan (al-Basri) said: By Allah, he did teach them the Book of their Lord, the Sunnah of their Prophet, fought alongside them against their enemy, and distributed the spoils among them. By Allah, no rider came upon them who was better for them than Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. Ibn Shudhab said: When he prayed Fajr, he would order the people to remain in their places, then he would go through the rows, man by man, teaching them the Quran until he had covered all the rows. Ibn Shudhab said: He entered (Basra) on a grey camel and left it on one when he was dismissed.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage contains a detailed report from al-Hasan al-Basri about Umar's commissioning of Abu Musa. Umar called him from Syria and gave him a choice between his own desire (jihad on the Syrian front) and Umar's need (teaching, fighting, and governing in Basra). Abu Musa accepted Umar's need. The mission included three tasks: teaching the Quran and Sunnah, fighting the enemy, and distributing the spoils of war (fay'). Al-Hasan testifies that Abu Musa fulfilled all three excellently, calling him the best rider (leader) to come to Basra. The report then adds a description from Ibn Shudhab of Abu Musa's routine: after Fajr prayer, he would have the congregation stay and he would go row by row teaching each man the Quran. The final detail notes that he entered Basra on a grey camel and left it on one when he was dismissed (from office), showing his simplicity and lack of material accumulation.

Key Terms
بعث
حاجتي
الجهاد
فئ
يقرئه القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Teaching was his only role.
Correction: Umar assigned him three duties: teaching, fighting, and distributing spoils; he fulfilled all.

Checkpoint
What three specific duties did Umar assign to Abu Musa in Basra?
A) Teach, lead prayers, judge
B) Teach Quran and Sunnah, fight enemy, distribute spoils
C) Teach, build mosques, collect taxes
D) Teach, issue fatwa, lead Hajj

Key Takeaway
Umar's appointment of Abu Musa demonstrates that a Muslim leader must combine teaching, military command, and just distribution of resources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Umar asked Abu Musa to prefer his need over his own, Abu Musa immediately complied. What is the 'need' that Allah is asking you to prefer over your personal desires today? Are you willing to set aside your ambitions to fulfill His command—whether it be teaching others, standing for truth, or serving your community?
?
Abu Musa personally taught each man the Quran row by row after Fajr, investing time and effort in every individual. How much personal attention do you give to learning and sharing the Quran with those around you? Will you commit to one concrete action this week to teach or recite the Quran with someone else, even if it costs you comfort or convenience?
?
Abu Musa entered Basra on a simple grey camel and left on one when dismissed, showing detachment from worldly status. How attached are you to your possessions, position, or reputation? If Allah called you to leave everything behind for His sake tomorrow, would you be able to do so without hesitation?

Al-Ash'ari's Principle: Refutation, Not Initiation
Ibn Khafif's question and al-Ash'ari's answer about initiating debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's methodological principle of only engaging in debate when others speak falsely, and why he considered it an obligation.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari never started debates; he only refuted falsehood when it appeared.

This lesson presents a vivid anecdote from Ibn Khafif, who traveled to meet al-Ash'ari and witnessed his approach firsthand. Through a direct question and answer, al-Ash'ari reveals the core of his methodology: he does not initiate theological discussions but responds when others speak what is impermissible in Islam. This story grounds his approach in the duty to defend the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the full story of Ibn Khafif's meeting with al-Ash'ari. Notice how Ibn Khafif first mistakes al-Ash'ari for a stranger, then observes him refute a speaker, and finally asks him directly about his method.
The story contains multiple layers - pay attention to the key question and answer.
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's principle
Focus on al-Ash'ari's response to Ibn Khafif's question: 'Why do you not ask while others ask?' Identify the exact phrasing he uses to explain his stance.
The phrase 'لا نكلم هؤلاء ابتداء' is the key.
3
Understand the rationale
Al-Ash'ari grounds his approach in obligation: he refutes only when others 'delve into what is not permissible' because Allah has made refuting those who oppose the truth obligatory. Reflect on why this is a defensive rather than offensive stance.
This connects to the broader Sunni view that kalam is a defensive tool.
4
Apply to misconceptions
Consider the common misunderstanding that al-Ash'ari was an aggressive debater. How does this story correct that? Discuss why his method is based on necessity, not preference.
This principle shaped the later Ash'ari school's approach to theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبدالله محمد ابن الفضل بن احمد الفقيه الفراوى انا الأستاذ أبو القاسم عبدالكريم بن شوارن القشيري قال سمعت الشيخ ابا عبدالله محمد بن عبدالله بن عبید الله الشيرازي الصوفى يقول سمعت بعض أصحاب أبي عبيدالله يقول سمعت أبا عبد الله بن خفيف يقول دخلت البصرة في أيام شبابي لأرى أبا الحسن الأشعرى لما بلغني خبره فرأیت شیخا بهى النظر فقلت له أين منزل أبي الحسن الأشعرى فقال وما الذي تريد منه فقلت أحب أن ألقاه فقال ابتكر غدا إلى هذا الموضع قال فابتكرت فلما رأيته تبعته فدخل دار بعض وجوه البلد فلما أبصروه اكرموا محله، وكان هناك العلماء ومجلس نظر فأقعدوه فى الصدر، ثم إنه سأل بعضهم مسألة فلما شرع في الكلام دخل هذا الشيخ فأخذ يرد عليه ويناظره حتى أفجمه فقضيت العجب علمه وفصاحته، فقلت لبعض من كان عنده من هذا الشيخ فقال أبو الحسن الأشعرى، فلما قاموا تبعته فالتفت إلى وقال يافتي كيف رأيت الأشعرى فخدمته وقلت ياسيدى كما هو في محله ولكن مسألة فقال ما هي فقلت مثلك في فضلك وعلو منزلتك كيف لم تسأل ويسأل غيرك، فقال إنا لا نكلم هؤلاء ابتداء ولكن اذا خاضوا في ذكر ما لا يجوز في دين الله رددنا عليهم بحكم ما فرض الله سبحانه وتعالى علينا من الرد على مخالفي الحق
And the shaykh Abu 'Abdillah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Ahmad al-Faqih al-Farawi informed us, he said: the master Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Karim ibn Shawran al-Qushayri informed us, he said: I heard the shaykh Abu 'Abdillah Muhammad ibn 'Abdillah ibn 'Ubaydillah al-Shirazi al-Sufi say: I heard some of the companions of Abu 'Ubaydillah say: I heard Abu 'Abdillah ibn Khafif say: I entered Basra in my youth to see Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari when his news reached me. I saw a shaykh of handsome appearance and said to him: 'Where is the house of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari?' He said: 'What do you want from him?' I said: 'I would like to meet him.' He said: 'Come early tomorrow to this place.' He said: So I came early. Then I saw him and followed him. He entered the house of some notable of the city. When they saw him, they honored his station. There were scholars there and a study circle. They seated him at the head. Then he asked someone a question. When he began to speak, this shaykh (i.e., al-Ash'ari) entered and began to refute him and debate him until he silenced him. I was amazed at his knowledge and eloquence. I said to someone who was with him: 'Who is this shaykh?' He said: 'Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.' When they stood, I followed him. He turned to me and said: 'O lad, how did you see al-Ash'ari?' I served him and said: 'O my master, he is as he is in his station, but I have a question.' He said: 'What is it?' I said: 'Someone like you, with your virtue and high station, how is it that you do not ask questions while others ask?' He said: 'We do not speak to these people initially, but when they delve into mentioning what is not permissible in the religion of Allah, we refute them by virtue of what Allah the Exalted and Majestic has made obligatory upon us of refuting those who oppose the truth.'
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a second anecdote from Ibn Khafif, where he initially does not recognize al-Ash'ari. The shaykh he first meets is actually al-Ash'ari himself, who directs him to come early. Ibn Khafif then witnesses al-Ash'ari refute a speaker in a gathering, silencing him. After the gathering, Ibn Khafif follows al-Ash'ari and asks why he, with his knowledge, does not ask questions (i.e., initiate debates) while others ask. Al-Ash'ari's response articulates his methodological principle: he does not initiate discussion with the Mu'tazila or others; rather, he only responds when they themselves bring up matters that are not permissible in Islam. He frames this as an obligation from Allah to defend the truth against those who oppose it. This highlights the defensive nature of kalam according to al-Ash'ari.

Key Terms
الرد على مخالفي الحق
ابتداء
الخوض
أفحمه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari was an aggressive debater who initiated conflicts.
Correction: This anecdote shows he only refuted when others first spoke falsely; he did not initiate debate.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari in this passage, why does he not initiate debates?
A) He does not know how
B) He is shy
C) He only refutes when others speak of what is not permissible in religion
D) He does not like to argue

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's theological method was reactive: he only engaged in debate to refute falsehood when it was first spoken, fulfilling the divine obligation to defend the truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone speak falsely about Islam, do you feel the weight of a divine obligation to respond, or do you stay silent out of comfort? What would change if you truly believed it was Allah's command to refute falsehood?
?
Al-Ash'ari did not seek out arguments—he only reacted when falsehood was raised. In your own life, where do you initiate unnecessary arguments or debates that do not stem from a genuine need to defend the truth? How can you adopt a more reactive, principled approach?
?
If you were in Ibn Khafif's place and heard al-Ash'ari's response, would your heart be satisfied with 'because Allah made it obligatory' as the only reason to speak? Or do you secretly wish for a more glamorous, proactive role? Examine your intentions: do you defend the truth for Allah, or for your own ego?

The Three Types of Sunni Scholars
Ibn Asakir enumerates three types of scholars by specialization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of Sunni scholars based on their primary focus: fiqh, usul, or both, and how these categories represent legitimate specialization within Ahl al-Sunnah.

Core Idea
Sunni scholars differ in specialization but agree on fundamentals.

Ibn Asakir begins by noting that despite complete agreement on the principles of faith, scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah have different emphases. Some devote themselves to deriving legal rulings from the revealed sources, others to mastering the proofs of theology (usul), and a third group excels in both. This passage introduces these three types, illustrating the third with the example of the Ash'aris of Yemen who came to the Prophet seeking both fiqh and answers about the beginning of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Ibn Asakir first states the common ground (agreement on fundamentals) before listing the three types.
The three types are described in quick succession.
2
Identify each type
As you read, identify the three types: those focused on fiqh, those focused on usul, and those who combine both. Look for the keywords 'قصر همته على التفقه' and 'قصر همته على التبحر في دلائل الأصول'.
The third type is introduced with 'ومنهم من جعل همته فيهما جميعا'.
3
Reflect on the Ash'ari example
Consider why Ibn Asakir mentions the Ash'aris of Yemen as an example of those who combine both. Think about the significance of their question to the Prophet about the beginning of creation.
This shows that concern for theology is not new; it dates back to the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وعلماء هذه الأمة من أهل السنة والجماعة في الاشتغال بالعلم مع الاتفاق في أصول الدين على اضرب منهم من قصر همته على التفقه في الدين بدلائله الحججه من التفسير والحديث والاجماع والقياس دون التبحر في دلائل الأصول ومنهم من قصر همته على التبحر في دلائل الأصول دون التبحر في لائل الفقه ومنهم من جعل همته فيهما جميعا كما فعل الأشعريون من اهل اليمن حيث قالوا لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اتيناك لنتـفـقـه في الدين ولنسألك عن أول هذا الأمر كيف كان وفى ذلك تصديق ما روى عن المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم ( اختلاف امتی رحمة ) كما سمعت من الشيخ الامام أبي الفتح ناصر بن الحسن العمري قال : سمعت الشيخ الإمام أبا بكر القفال المروزى رحمه الله يقول : معناه اختلاف هممهم رحمة یعنى فهمـة واحد تكون في الفقه وهمة آخر تكون في الكلام، كما تختلف همم اصحاب الحرف في حرفهم ليقوم كل واحد منهم مصالح العباد والبلاد ثم كل من جعل همته في معرفة دلائل الفقه لم ينكر فى نفسه ماذهب إليه أهل الأصول منهم بل ذهب في اعتقاد الذهب مذهبهم بأقل ما دله على صحته من الحجج إلا أنه رأى ان اشتغاله بذلك أنفع وأولى، ومن صرف همته منهم إلى معرفة دلائل الأصول وحججه ذهب في الفروع مذهب احد الأئمة الذين سميناهم من فقهاء الأمصار إلا أنه رأى ان اشتغاله بذلك عند ظهور البدع انفع وأحرى فعلماء السنة اذن مـجـتـمـعـون والأشعريون منهم الجمـاعـتـهـم في علم الأصول موافقون
This, and the scholars of this community from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, in their occupation with knowledge while agreeing on the fundamentals of the religion, are of types: among them is one who confined his concern to deep understanding in religion with its proofs and evidences from tafsir, hadith, ijma', and qiyas without delving into the evidences of the fundamentals; and among them is one who confined his concern to delving into the evidences of the fundamentals without delving into the evidences of fiqh; and among them is one who made his concern in both of them together, as did the Ash'aris from the people of Yemen when they said to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'We have come to you to gain deep understanding in religion and to ask you about the beginning of this matter, how it was.' And in that is confirmation of what is narrated from the Chosen One, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'The difference of my community is a mercy.' As I heard from the shaykh al-imam Abu al-Fath Nasir ibn al-Hasan al-'Umari; he said: I heard the shaykh al-imam Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Marwazi, may Allah have mercy on him, say: Its meaning is the difference of their concerns is a mercy—meaning one concern is in fiqh and another concern is in kalam, just as the concerns of the people of professions differ in their professions, so that each of them may undertake the interests of the servants and the lands. Then, each one who makes his concern in knowing the evidences of fiqh does not deny in himself what the people of fundamentals among them have gone to; rather, he goes in belief of the madhhab [or 'gold'?] to their madhhab with the least of the proofs that indicate its correctness, except that he saw that his occupation with that is more beneficial and more appropriate. And the one who turns his concern among them to knowing the evidences of the fundamentals and their proofs goes in the branches to the madhhab of one of the imams whom we mentioned among the jurists of the regions, except that he saw that his occupation with that at the time of the appearance of innovations is more beneficial and more fitting. So the scholars of the Sunnah are therefore in agreement, and the Ash'aris among them are in agreement regarding the science of fundamentals.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a classification of Sunni scholars according to their scholarly focus. Ibn Asakir states that despite agreeing on the fundamentals of religion, scholars differ in their specialization. He identifies three types: (1) those who focus on fiqh (deep understanding) using proofs from tafsir, hadith, ijma', and qiyas without delving into the evidences of usul (fundamentals); (2) those who focus on the evidences of usul without delving into the evidences of fiqh; (3) those who combine both, like the Ash'aris of Yemen who came to the Prophet asking both for fiqh and about the beginning of creation. This distinction is supported by the hadith 'ikhtilaf ummati rahma' (difference of my community is a mercy), which Ibn Asakir interprets via al-Qaffal as referring to the difference of their concerns (himam) – one concern for fiqh and one for kalam – like the differing professions that serve the welfare of people and lands. The passage then explains that each type does not reject the other; those focused on fiqh accept the usul position with minimal proof, considering fiqh more beneficial for themselves, while those focused on usul follow one of the four imams in furu' (branches) but see their specialization as more beneficial especially when innovations appear. Thus, all Sunni scholars are united, and the Ash'aris are in agreement with others on usul.

Key Terms
الفقه
الأصول
الهمة
أهل السنة والجماعة
الأشعريون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, which type of scholar is exemplified by the Ash'aris of Yemen?
A) Those focused only on fiqh
B) Those focused only on usul
C) Those focused on both fiqh and usul
D) Those who reject both fiqh and usul

Key Takeaway
The three categories show that specialization within Sunni scholarship is legitimate and does not compromise unity in fundamentals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the scholars 'agree on the fundamentals of religion,' do you genuinely prioritize unity on core beliefs over secondary disagreements with fellow Muslims? What steps can you take to strengthen that unity in your own circle?
?
The Ash'aris of Yemen came to the Prophet seeking both 'deep understanding in religion' (fiqh) and knowledge of 'the beginning of this matter' (theology). Which of these two do you instinctively neglect, and how will you intentionally pursue a more balanced approach starting this week?
?
Ibn Asakir describes three legitimate paths of specialization. Have you ever looked down on a scholar or student of knowledge because they focused on a different area than you? How can you correct that attitude and instead value the diversity within Ahl al-Sunnah?

The Meaning of 'Ikhtilaf Ummati Rahma'
Interpretation of the hadith of difference as diversity of specializations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretation of the hadith 'Ikhtilaf Ummati Rahma' as referring to diversity of scholarly concerns, not contradictory beliefs.

Core Idea
Diversity of specializations is a mercy, not disagreement in doctrine.

Ibn Asakir now connects the classification of scholars to the well-known hadith 'The difference of my community is a mercy.' He reports an interpretation from al-Qaffal al-Marwazi that explains this 'difference' as the variety of concerns (himam) among scholars—one devoted to fiqh, another to kalam—similar to how different professions serve society. This lesson unpacks that interpretation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its interpretation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that Ibn Asakir first states that the classification confirms the hadith, then narrates the interpretation via two trustworthy shaykhs.
The chain of narration adds authority.
2
Understand al-Qaffal's analogy
Al-Qaffal says 'difference of their concerns is a mercy' and compares it to the differing concerns of craftsmen. Think about how different professions serve the community.
The analogy highlights that specialization is beneficial, not divisive.
3
Connect to the earlier classification
Reflect on how this interpretation ties back to the three types of scholars. The 'difference' is not in beliefs but in the areas of focus.
This resolves a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This, and the scholars of this community from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, in their occupation with knowledge while agreeing on the fundamentals of the religion, are of types: among them is one who confined his concern to deep understanding in religion with its proofs and evidences from tafsir, hadith, ijma', and qiyas without delving into the evidences of the fundamentals; and among them is one who confined his concern to delving into the evidences of the fundamentals without delving into the evidences of fiqh; and among them is one who made his concern in both of them together, as did the Ash'aris from the people of Yemen when they said to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'We have come to you to gain deep understanding in religion and to ask you about the beginning of this matter, how it was.' Misconception & Correction

According to al-Qaffal's interpretation cited in the passage, what does 'ikhtilaf ummati rahma' mean?
A) Different legal rulings are a mercy
B) Different concerns among scholars are a mercy
C) Different sects are all correct
D) Disagreement in fundamentals is allowed

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Ikhtilaf ummati rahma' is best understood as a mercy of diverse specializations, not a license for doctrinal division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The hadith calls diversity of concerns a mercy. Identify one specialization within the Muslim community that you have undervalued. What concrete step will you take this week to benefit from that specialty or appreciate its contribution?

Unity of Sunni Scholars Despite Specialization
Explanation of mutual respect and unity among Sunni scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Sunni scholars of different specializations respect each other's contributions and remain united in fundamentals, especially the Ash'aris.

Core Idea
Different focuses, one community: Sunni scholars are united in creed.

Having presented the three types and the hadith interpretation, Ibn Asakir concludes this section by emphasizing that scholars of fiqh do not reject the specialists in usul, and vice versa. Each recognizes the value of the other's work and follows the same core beliefs. The Ash'aris, in particular, are in full agreement with other Sunnis on the principles of religion.

Guided Path
1
Read how fiqh scholars view usul scholars
Read the first part of the passage. Notice that the fiqh scholar does not deny the usul position; he accepts it with minimal proofs but considers his own specialization more beneficial.
This shows mutual respect.
2
Read how usul scholars view fiqh scholars
Read the second part. The usul specialist follows one of the four imams in furu' and sees his specialization as more beneficial when innovations appear.
Both recognize the other's validity.
3
Conclude with the unity of Ahl al-Sunnah
Read the final sentence. Ibn Asakir states that Sunni scholars are in agreement, and the Ash'aris among them are in agreement regarding the science of fundamentals.
This reinforces the theme of unity despite specialization.

هذا وعلماء هذه الأمة من أهل السنة والجماعة في الاشتغال بالعلم مع الاتفاق في أصول الدين على اضرب منهم من قصر همته على التفقه في الدين بدلائله الحججه من التفسير والحديث والاجماع والقياس دون التبحر في دلائل الأصول ومنهم من قصر همته على التبحر في دلائل الأصول دون التبحر في لائل الفقه ومنهم من جعل همته فيهما جميعا كما فعل الأشعريون من اهل اليمن حيث قالوا لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اتيناك لنتـفـقـه في الدين ولنسألك عن أول هذا الأمر كيف كان وفى ذلك تصديق ما روى عن المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم ( اختلاف امتی رحمة ) كما سمعت من الشيخ الامام أبي الفتح ناصر بن الحسن العمري قال : سمعت الشيخ الإمام أبا بكر القفال المروزى رحمه الله يقول : معناه اختلاف هممهم رحمة یعنى فهمـة واحد تكون في الفقه وهمة آخر تكون في الكلام، كما تختلف همم اصحاب الحرف في حرفهم ليقوم كل واحد منهم مصالح العباد والبلاد ثم كل من جعل همته في معرفة دلائل الفقه لم ينكر فى نفسه ماذهب إليه أهل الأصول منهم بل ذهب في اعتقاد الذهب مذهبهم بأقل ما دله على صحته من الحجج إلا أنه رأى ان اشتغاله بذلك أنفع وأولى، ومن صرف همته منهم إلى معرفة دلائل الأصول وحججه ذهب في الفروع مذهب احد الأئمة الذين سميناهم من فقهاء الأمصار إلا أنه رأى ان اشتغاله بذلك عند ظهور البدع انفع وأحرى فعلماء السنة اذن مـجـتـمـعـون والأشعريون منهم الجمـاعـتـهـم في علم الأصول موافقون
This, and the scholars of this community from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, in their occupation with knowledge while agreeing on the fundamentals of the religion, are of types: among them is one who confined his concern to deep understanding in religion with its proofs and evidences from tafsir, hadith, ijma', and qiyas without delving into the evidences of the fundamentals; and among them is one who confined his concern to delving into the evidences of the fundamentals without delving into the evidences of fiqh; and among them is one who made his concern in both of them together, as did the Ash'aris from the people of Yemen when they said to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'We have come to you to gain deep understanding in religion and to ask you about the beginning of this matter, how it was.' And in that is confirmation of what is narrated from the Chosen One, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'The difference of my community is a mercy.' As I heard from the shaykh al-imam Abu al-Fath Nasir ibn al-Hasan al-'Umari; he said: I heard the shaykh al-imam Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Marwazi, may Allah have mercy on him, say: Its meaning is the difference of their concerns is a mercy—meaning one concern is in fiqh and another concern is in kalam, just as the concerns of the people of professions differ in their professions, so that each of them may undertake the interests of the servants and the lands. Then, each one who makes his concern in knowing the evidences of fiqh does not deny in himself what the people of fundamentals among them have gone to; rather, he goes in belief of the madhhab [or 'gold'?] to their madhhab with the least of the proofs that indicate its correctness, except that he saw that his occupation with that is more beneficial and more appropriate. And the one who turns his concern among them to knowing the evidences of the fundamentals and their proofs goes in the branches to the madhhab of one of the imams whom we mentioned among the jurists of the regions, except that he saw that his occupation with that at the time of the appearance of innovations is more beneficial and more fitting. So the scholars of the Sunnah are therefore in agreement, and the Ash'aris among them are in agreement regarding the science of fundamentals.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a classification of Sunni scholars according to their scholarly focus. Ibn Asakir states that despite agreeing on the fundamentals of religion, scholars differ in their specialization. He identifies three types: (1) those who focus on fiqh (deep understanding) using proofs from tafsir, hadith, ijma', and qiyas without delving into the evidences of usul (fundamentals); (2) those who focus on the evidences of usul without delving into the evidences of fiqh; (3) those who combine both, like the Ash'aris of Yemen who came to the Prophet asking both for fiqh and about the beginning of creation. This distinction is supported by the hadith 'ikhtilaf ummati rahma' (difference of my community is a mercy), which Ibn Asakir interprets via al-Qaffal as referring to the difference of their concerns (himam) – one concern for fiqh and one for kalam – like the differing professions that serve the welfare of people and lands. The passage then explains that each type does not reject the other; those focused on fiqh accept the usul position with minimal proof, considering fiqh more beneficial for themselves, while those focused on usul follow one of the four imams in furu' (branches) but see their specialization as more beneficial especially when innovations appear. Thus, all Sunni scholars are united, and the Ash'aris are in agreement with others on usul.

Key Terms
دلائل الفقه
دلائل الأصول
الأئمة
فقهاء الأمصار
البدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Asakir say about the fiqh specialist's view of the usul position?
A) He completely rejects it
B) He follows it with minimal proofs
C) He is indifferent to it
D) He only follows it in times of innovation

Key Takeaway
Despite different academic focuses, Sunni scholars are united in fundamentals, and the Ash'aris are part of this unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a scholar whose specialization differs from your own interests, do you feel a sense of unity with them or a sense of distance? What steps can you take to actively foster respect for all Sunni scholars, regardless of their focus?
?
The passage teaches that each scholar considers their own work more beneficial in certain contexts. Which area of Islamic knowledge do you feel is most needed today, and how can you contribute without looking down on those who choose a different path?
?
If you ever find yourself favoring one school of thought over another, does that affect your belief in the unity of the Sunni community? How can you ensure that your preferences do not lead to division in your heart?
